<s> he was a tall and portly gentleman with a hooked nose and a profusion of curling brown hair and whiskers his coat was covered with the richest frogs braiding and velvet <\s>

<s> william minns without whom he never travelled <\s>

<s> dolphin's appearance in the remote little chatteris theatre may be accounted for in this manner <\s>
<s> in spite of all his exertions and the perpetual blazes of triumph coruscations of talent victories of good old english comedy which his play bills advertised his theatre which if you please and to injure no present susceptibilities and vested interests we shall call the museum theatre by no means prospered and the famous impresario found himself on the verge of ruin <\s>
<s> the great hubbard had acted legitimate drama for twenty nights and failed to remunerate anybody but himself the celebrated mr <\s>
<s> cawdor had come out in mr <\s>
<s> rawhead's tragedy and in their favourite round of pieces and had not attracted the public <\s>
<s> herr garbage's lions and tigers had drawn for a little time until one of the animals had bitten a piece out of the herr's shoulder when the lord chamberlain interfered and put a stop to this species of performance and the grand lyrical drama though brought out with unexampled splendour and success with monsieur poumons as first tenor and an enormous orchestra had almost crushed poor dolphin in its triumphant progress so that great as his genius and resources were they seemed to be at an end <\s>
<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>
<s> one of the illustrious patrons of the museum theatre and occupant of the great proscenium box was a gentleman whose name has been mentioned in a previous history that refined patron of the arts and enlightened lover of music and the drama the most noble the marquis of steyne <\s>
<s> his lordship's avocations as a statesman prevented him from attending the playhouse very often or coming very early <\s>
<s> but he occasionally appeared at the theatre in time for the ballet and was always received with the greatest respect by the manager from whom he sometimes condescended to receive a visit in his box <\s>
<s> it communicated with the stage and when anything occurred there which particularly pleased him when a new face made its appearance among the coryphees or a fair dancer executed a pas with especial grace or agility mr <\s>
<s> wagg or some other aide de camp of the noble marquis would be commissioned to go behind the scenes and express the great man's approbation or make the inquiries which were prompted by his lordship's curiosity or his interest in the dramatic art <\s>
<s> he could not be seen by the audience for lord steyne sate modestly behind a curtain and looked only towards the stage but you could know he was in the house by the glances which all the corps de ballet and all the principal dancers cast towards his box <\s>
<s> i have seen many scores of pairs of eyes as in the palm dance in the ballet of cook at otaheite where no less than a hundred and twenty lovely female savages in palm leaves and feather aprons were made to dance round floridor as captain cook ogling that box as they performed before it and have often wondered to remark the presence of mind of mademoiselle sauterelle or mademoiselle de bondi known as la petite caoutchoue who when actually up in the air quivering like so many shuttlecocks always kept their lovely eyes winking at that box in which the great steyne sate <\s>
<s> now and then you would hear a harsh voice from behind the curtain cry brava brava or a pair of white gloves wave from it and begin to applaud <\s>
<s> bondi or sauterelle when they came down to earth curtsied and smiled especially to those hands before they walked up the stage again panting and happy <\s>

<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>
<s> one of the illustrious patrons of the museum theatre and occupant of the great proscenium box was a gentleman whose name has been mentioned in a previous history that refined patron of the arts and enlightened lover of music and the drama the most noble the marquis of steyne <\s>
<s> his lordship's avocations as a statesman prevented him from attending the playhouse very often or coming very early <\s>
<s> but he occasionally appeared at the theatre in time for the ballet and was always received with the greatest respect by the manager from whom he sometimes condescended to receive a visit in his box <\s>
<s> it communicated with the stage and when anything occurred there which particularly pleased him when a new face made its appearance among the coryphees or a fair dancer executed a pas with especial grace or agility mr <\s>

<s> he could not be seen by the audience for lord steyne sate modestly behind a curtain and looked only towards the stage but you could know he was in the house by the glances which all the corps de ballet and all the principal dancers cast towards his box <\s>

<s> now and then you would hear a harsh voice from behind the curtain cry brava brava or a pair of white gloves wave from it and begin to applaud <\s>
<s> bondi or sauterelle when they came down to earth curtsied and smiled especially to those hands before they walked up the stage again panting and happy <\s>
<s> one night this great prince surrounded by a few choice friends was in his box at the museum and they were making such a noise and laughter that the pit was scandalised and many indignant voices were bawling out silence so loudly that wagg wondered the police did not interfere to take the rascals out <\s>

<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>
<s> one of the illustrious patrons of the museum theatre and occupant of the great proscenium box was a gentleman whose name has been mentioned in a previous history that refined patron of the arts and enlightened lover of music and the drama the most noble the marquis of steyne <\s>
<s> his lordship's avocations as a statesman prevented him from attending the playhouse very often or coming very early <\s>
<s> but he occasionally appeared at the theatre in time for the ballet and was always received with the greatest respect by the manager from whom he sometimes condescended to receive a visit in his box <\s>

<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>
<s> one of the illustrious patrons of the museum theatre and occupant of the great proscenium box was a gentleman whose name has been mentioned in a previous history that refined patron of the arts and enlightened lover of music and the drama the most noble the marquis of steyne <\s>
<s> his lordship's avocations as a statesman prevented him from attending the playhouse very often or coming very early <\s>
<s> but he occasionally appeared at the theatre in time for the ballet and was always received with the greatest respect by the manager from whom he sometimes condescended to receive a visit in his box <\s>
<s> it communicated with the stage and when anything occurred there which particularly pleased him when a new face made its appearance among the coryphees or a fair dancer executed a pas with especial grace or agility mr <\s>

<s> he could not be seen by the audience for lord steyne sate modestly behind a curtain and looked only towards the stage but you could know he was in the house by the glances which all the corps de ballet and all the principal dancers cast towards his box <\s>

<s> bondi or sauterelle when they came down to earth curtsied and smiled especially to those hands before they walked up the stage again panting and happy <\s>

<s> it communicated with the stage and when anything occurred there which particularly pleased him when a new face made its appearance among the coryphees or a fair dancer executed a pas with especial grace or agility mr <\s>
<s> wagg or some other aide de camp of the noble marquis would be commissioned to go behind the scenes and express the great man's approbation or make the inquiries which were prompted by his lordship's curiosity or his interest in the dramatic art <\s>
<s> he could not be seen by the audience for lord steyne sate modestly behind a curtain and looked only towards the stage but you could know he was in the house by the glances which all the corps de ballet and all the principal dancers cast towards his box <\s>
<s> i have seen many scores of pairs of eyes as in the palm dance in the ballet of cook at otaheite where no less than a hundred and twenty lovely female savages in palm leaves and feather aprons were made to dance round floridor as captain cook ogling that box as they performed before it and have often wondered to remark the presence of mind of mademoiselle sauterelle or mademoiselle de bondi known as la petite caoutchoue who when actually up in the air quivering like so many shuttlecocks always kept their lovely eyes winking at that box in which the great steyne sate <\s>
<s> now and then you would hear a harsh voice from behind the curtain cry brava brava or a pair of white gloves wave from it and begin to applaud <\s>
<s> bondi or sauterelle when they came down to earth curtsied and smiled especially to those hands before they walked up the stage again panting and happy <\s>
<s> one night this great prince surrounded by a few choice friends was in his box at the museum and they were making such a noise and laughter that the pit was scandalised and many indignant voices were bawling out silence so loudly that wagg wondered the police did not interfere to take the rascals out <\s>

<s> it is a delicate matter entre nous une affaire de coeur <\s>

<s> there he sate then miserable and gazing at her <\s>
<s> and she took no more notice of him than he did of the rest of the house <\s>

<s> she spouted to admiration the few words of her part and looked it still better <\s>
<s> the eyes which had overthrown pen's soul rolled and gleamed as lustrous as ever but it was not to him that they were directed that night <\s>

<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>

<s> his lordship's avocations as a statesman prevented him from attending the playhouse very often or coming very early <\s>
<s> but he occasionally appeared at the theatre in time for the ballet and was always received with the greatest respect by the manager from whom he sometimes condescended to receive a visit in his box <\s>

<s> he was a tall and portly gentleman with a hooked nose and a profusion of curling brown hair and whiskers his coat was covered with the richest frogs braiding and velvet <\s>

<s> william minns without whom he never travelled <\s>

<s> dolphin's appearance in the remote little chatteris theatre may be accounted for in this manner <\s>
<s> in spite of all his exertions and the perpetual blazes of triumph coruscations of talent victories of good old english comedy which his play bills advertised his theatre which if you please and to injure no present susceptibilities and vested interests we shall call the museum theatre by no means prospered and the famous impresario found himself on the verge of ruin <\s>
<s> the great hubbard had acted legitimate drama for twenty nights and failed to remunerate anybody but himself the celebrated mr <\s>
<s> cawdor had come out in mr <\s>
<s> rawhead's tragedy and in their favourite round of pieces and had not attracted the public <\s>
<s> herr garbage's lions and tigers had drawn for a little time until one of the animals had bitten a piece out of the herr's shoulder when the lord chamberlain interfered and put a stop to this species of performance and the grand lyrical drama though brought out with unexampled splendour and success with monsieur poumons as first tenor and an enormous orchestra had almost crushed poor dolphin in its triumphant progress so that great as his genius and resources were they seemed to be at an end <\s>
<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>
<s> one of the illustrious patrons of the museum theatre and occupant of the great proscenium box was a gentleman whose name has been mentioned in a previous history that refined patron of the arts and enlightened lover of music and the drama the most noble the marquis of steyne <\s>
<s> his lordship's avocations as a statesman prevented him from attending the playhouse very often or coming very early <\s>
<s> but he occasionally appeared at the theatre in time for the ballet and was always received with the greatest respect by the manager from whom he sometimes condescended to receive a visit in his box <\s>
<s> it communicated with the stage and when anything occurred there which particularly pleased him when a new face made its appearance among the coryphees or a fair dancer executed a pas with especial grace or agility mr <\s>
<s> wagg or some other aide de camp of the noble marquis would be commissioned to go behind the scenes and express the great man's approbation or make the inquiries which were prompted by his lordship's curiosity or his interest in the dramatic art <\s>
<s> he could not be seen by the audience for lord steyne sate modestly behind a curtain and looked only towards the stage but you could know he was in the house by the glances which all the corps de ballet and all the principal dancers cast towards his box <\s>
<s> i have seen many scores of pairs of eyes as in the palm dance in the ballet of cook at otaheite where no less than a hundred and twenty lovely female savages in palm leaves and feather aprons were made to dance round floridor as captain cook ogling that box as they performed before it and have often wondered to remark the presence of mind of mademoiselle sauterelle or mademoiselle de bondi known as la petite caoutchoue who when actually up in the air quivering like so many shuttlecocks always kept their lovely eyes winking at that box in which the great steyne sate <\s>
<s> now and then you would hear a harsh voice from behind the curtain cry brava brava or a pair of white gloves wave from it and begin to applaud <\s>
<s> bondi or sauterelle when they came down to earth curtsied and smiled especially to those hands before they walked up the stage again panting and happy <\s>
<s> one night this great prince surrounded by a few choice friends was in his box at the museum and they were making such a noise and laughter that the pit was scandalised and many indignant voices were bawling out silence so loudly that wagg wondered the police did not interfere to take the rascals out <\s>
<s> wenham was amusing the party in the box with extracts from a private letter which he had received from major pendennis whose absence in the country at the full london season had been remarked and of course deplored by his friends <\s>

<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>
<s> one of the illustrious patrons of the museum theatre and occupant of the great proscenium box was a gentleman whose name has been mentioned in a previous history that refined patron of the arts and enlightened lover of music and the drama the most noble the marquis of steyne <\s>
<s> his lordship's avocations as a statesman prevented him from attending the playhouse very often or coming very early <\s>
<s> but he occasionally appeared at the theatre in time for the ballet and was always received with the greatest respect by the manager from whom he sometimes condescended to receive a visit in his box <\s>
<s> it communicated with the stage and when anything occurred there which particularly pleased him when a new face made its appearance among the coryphees or a fair dancer executed a pas with especial grace or agility mr <\s>

<s> he could not be seen by the audience for lord steyne sate modestly behind a curtain and looked only towards the stage but you could know he was in the house by the glances which all the corps de ballet and all the principal dancers cast towards his box <\s>
<s> i have seen many scores of pairs of eyes as in the palm dance in the ballet of cook at otaheite where no less than a hundred and twenty lovely female savages in palm leaves and feather aprons were made to dance round floridor as captain cook ogling that box as they performed before it and have often wondered to remark the presence of mind of mademoiselle sauterelle or mademoiselle de bondi known as la petite caoutchoue who when actually up in the air quivering like so many shuttlecocks always kept their lovely eyes winking at that box in which the great steyne sate <\s>
<s> now and then you would hear a harsh voice from behind the curtain cry brava brava or a pair of white gloves wave from it and begin to applaud <\s>
<s> bondi or sauterelle when they came down to earth curtsied and smiled especially to those hands before they walked up the stage again panting and happy <\s>
<s> one night this great prince surrounded by a few choice friends was in his box at the museum and they were making such a noise and laughter that the pit was scandalised and many indignant voices were bawling out silence so loudly that wagg wondered the police did not interfere to take the rascals out <\s>

<s> william minns without whom he never travelled <\s>

<s> dolphin's appearance in the remote little chatteris theatre may be accounted for in this manner <\s>
<s> in spite of all his exertions and the perpetual blazes of triumph coruscations of talent victories of good old english comedy which his play bills advertised his theatre which if you please and to injure no present susceptibilities and vested interests we shall call the museum theatre by no means prospered and the famous impresario found himself on the verge of ruin <\s>
<s> the great hubbard had acted legitimate drama for twenty nights and failed to remunerate anybody but himself the celebrated mr <\s>
<s> cawdor had come out in mr <\s>
<s> rawhead's tragedy and in their favourite round of pieces and had not attracted the public <\s>
<s> herr garbage's lions and tigers had drawn for a little time until one of the animals had bitten a piece out of the herr's shoulder when the lord chamberlain interfered and put a stop to this species of performance and the grand lyrical drama though brought out with unexampled splendour and success with monsieur poumons as first tenor and an enormous orchestra had almost crushed poor dolphin in its triumphant progress so that great as his genius and resources were they seemed to be at an end <\s>
<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>
<s> one of the illustrious patrons of the museum theatre and occupant of the great proscenium box was a gentleman whose name has been mentioned in a previous history that refined patron of the arts and enlightened lover of music and the drama the most noble the marquis of steyne <\s>
<s> his lordship's avocations as a statesman prevented him from attending the playhouse very often or coming very early <\s>
<s> but he occasionally appeared at the theatre in time for the ballet and was always received with the greatest respect by the manager from whom he sometimes condescended to receive a visit in his box <\s>
<s> it communicated with the stage and when anything occurred there which particularly pleased him when a new face made its appearance among the coryphees or a fair dancer executed a pas with especial grace or agility mr <\s>
<s> wagg or some other aide de camp of the noble marquis would be commissioned to go behind the scenes and express the great man's approbation or make the inquiries which were prompted by his lordship's curiosity or his interest in the dramatic art <\s>
<s> he could not be seen by the audience for lord steyne sate modestly behind a curtain and looked only towards the stage but you could know he was in the house by the glances which all the corps de ballet and all the principal dancers cast towards his box <\s>
<s> i have seen many scores of pairs of eyes as in the palm dance in the ballet of cook at otaheite where no less than a hundred and twenty lovely female savages in palm leaves and feather aprons were made to dance round floridor as captain cook ogling that box as they performed before it and have often wondered to remark the presence of mind of mademoiselle sauterelle or mademoiselle de bondi known as la petite caoutchoue who when actually up in the air quivering like so many shuttlecocks always kept their lovely eyes winking at that box in which the great steyne sate <\s>
<s> now and then you would hear a harsh voice from behind the curtain cry brava brava or a pair of white gloves wave from it and begin to applaud <\s>
<s> bondi or sauterelle when they came down to earth curtsied and smiled especially to those hands before they walked up the stage again panting and happy <\s>
<s> one night this great prince surrounded by a few choice friends was in his box at the museum and they were making such a noise and laughter that the pit was scandalised and many indignant voices were bawling out silence so loudly that wagg wondered the police did not interfere to take the rascals out <\s>
<s> wenham was amusing the party in the box with extracts from a private letter which he had received from major pendennis whose absence in the country at the full london season had been remarked and of course deplored by his friends <\s>

<s> dolphin's appearance in the remote little chatteris theatre may be accounted for in this manner <\s>
<s> in spite of all his exertions and the perpetual blazes of triumph coruscations of talent victories of good old english comedy which his play bills advertised his theatre which if you please and to injure no present susceptibilities and vested interests we shall call the museum theatre by no means prospered and the famous impresario found himself on the verge of ruin <\s>
<s> the great hubbard had acted legitimate drama for twenty nights and failed to remunerate anybody but himself the celebrated mr <\s>
<s> cawdor had come out in mr <\s>
<s> rawhead's tragedy and in their favourite round of pieces and had not attracted the public <\s>

<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>
<s> one of the illustrious patrons of the museum theatre and occupant of the great proscenium box was a gentleman whose name has been mentioned in a previous history that refined patron of the arts and enlightened lover of music and the drama the most noble the marquis of steyne <\s>
<s> his lordship's avocations as a statesman prevented him from attending the playhouse very often or coming very early <\s>
<s> but he occasionally appeared at the theatre in time for the ballet and was always received with the greatest respect by the manager from whom he sometimes condescended to receive a visit in his box <\s>
<s> it communicated with the stage and when anything occurred there which particularly pleased him when a new face made its appearance among the coryphees or a fair dancer executed a pas with especial grace or agility mr <\s>
<s> wagg or some other aide de camp of the noble marquis would be commissioned to go behind the scenes and express the great man's approbation or make the inquiries which were prompted by his lordship's curiosity or his interest in the dramatic art <\s>
<s> he could not be seen by the audience for lord steyne sate modestly behind a curtain and looked only towards the stage but you could know he was in the house by the glances which all the corps de ballet and all the principal dancers cast towards his box <\s>
<s> i have seen many scores of pairs of eyes as in the palm dance in the ballet of cook at otaheite where no less than a hundred and twenty lovely female savages in palm leaves and feather aprons were made to dance round floridor as captain cook ogling that box as they performed before it and have often wondered to remark the presence of mind of mademoiselle sauterelle or mademoiselle de bondi known as la petite caoutchoue who when actually up in the air quivering like so many shuttlecocks always kept their lovely eyes winking at that box in which the great steyne sate <\s>
<s> now and then you would hear a harsh voice from behind the curtain cry brava brava or a pair of white gloves wave from it and begin to applaud <\s>
<s> bondi or sauterelle when they came down to earth curtsied and smiled especially to those hands before they walked up the stage again panting and happy <\s>
<s> one night this great prince surrounded by a few choice friends was in his box at the museum and they were making such a noise and laughter that the pit was scandalised and many indignant voices were bawling out silence so loudly that wagg wondered the police did not interfere to take the rascals out <\s>
<s> wenham was amusing the party in the box with extracts from a private letter which he had received from major pendennis whose absence in the country at the full london season had been remarked and of course deplored by his friends <\s>

<s> it is a delicate matter entre nous une affaire de coeur <\s>
<s> there is a young friend of mine who is gone wild about a certain miss fotheringay an actress at the theatre here and i must own to you as handsome a woman and as it appears to me as good an actress as ever put on rouge <\s>

<s> he had under waistcoats many splendid rings jewelled pins and neck chains <\s>

<s> in spite of all his exertions and the perpetual blazes of triumph coruscations of talent victories of good old english comedy which his play bills advertised his theatre which if you please and to injure no present susceptibilities and vested interests we shall call the museum theatre by no means prospered and the famous impresario found himself on the verge of ruin <\s>

<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>

<s> dolphin's appearance in the remote little chatteris theatre may be accounted for in this manner <\s>
<s> in spite of all his exertions and the perpetual blazes of triumph coruscations of talent victories of good old english comedy which his play bills advertised his theatre which if you please and to injure no present susceptibilities and vested interests we shall call the museum theatre by no means prospered and the famous impresario found himself on the verge of ruin <\s>

<s> rawhead's tragedy and in their favourite round of pieces and had not attracted the public <\s>

<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>
<s> one of the illustrious patrons of the museum theatre and occupant of the great proscenium box was a gentleman whose name has been mentioned in a previous history that refined patron of the arts and enlightened lover of music and the drama the most noble the marquis of steyne <\s>
<s> his lordship's avocations as a statesman prevented him from attending the playhouse very often or coming very early <\s>
<s> but he occasionally appeared at the theatre in time for the ballet and was always received with the greatest respect by the manager from whom he sometimes condescended to receive a visit in his box <\s>
<s> it communicated with the stage and when anything occurred there which particularly pleased him when a new face made its appearance among the coryphees or a fair dancer executed a pas with especial grace or agility mr <\s>
<s> wagg or some other aide de camp of the noble marquis would be commissioned to go behind the scenes and express the great man's approbation or make the inquiries which were prompted by his lordship's curiosity or his interest in the dramatic art <\s>
<s> he could not be seen by the audience for lord steyne sate modestly behind a curtain and looked only towards the stage but you could know he was in the house by the glances which all the corps de ballet and all the principal dancers cast towards his box <\s>
<s> i have seen many scores of pairs of eyes as in the palm dance in the ballet of cook at otaheite where no less than a hundred and twenty lovely female savages in palm leaves and feather aprons were made to dance round floridor as captain cook ogling that box as they performed before it and have often wondered to remark the presence of mind of mademoiselle sauterelle or mademoiselle de bondi known as la petite caoutchoue who when actually up in the air quivering like so many shuttlecocks always kept their lovely eyes winking at that box in which the great steyne sate <\s>

<s> bondi or sauterelle when they came down to earth curtsied and smiled especially to those hands before they walked up the stage again panting and happy <\s>

<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>
<s> one of the illustrious patrons of the museum theatre and occupant of the great proscenium box was a gentleman whose name has been mentioned in a previous history that refined patron of the arts and enlightened lover of music and the drama the most noble the marquis of steyne <\s>
<s> his lordship's avocations as a statesman prevented him from attending the playhouse very often or coming very early <\s>
<s> but he occasionally appeared at the theatre in time for the ballet and was always received with the greatest respect by the manager from whom he sometimes condescended to receive a visit in his box <\s>
<s> it communicated with the stage and when anything occurred there which particularly pleased him when a new face made its appearance among the coryphees or a fair dancer executed a pas with especial grace or agility mr <\s>

<s> he could not be seen by the audience for lord steyne sate modestly behind a curtain and looked only towards the stage but you could know he was in the house by the glances which all the corps de ballet and all the principal dancers cast towards his box <\s>

<s> now and then you would hear a harsh voice from behind the curtain cry brava brava or a pair of white gloves wave from it and begin to applaud <\s>
<s> bondi or sauterelle when they came down to earth curtsied and smiled especially to those hands before they walked up the stage again panting and happy <\s>
<s> one night this great prince surrounded by a few choice friends was in his box at the museum and they were making such a noise and laughter that the pit was scandalised and many indignant voices were bawling out silence so loudly that wagg wondered the police did not interfere to take the rascals out <\s>

<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>
<s> one of the illustrious patrons of the museum theatre and occupant of the great proscenium box was a gentleman whose name has been mentioned in a previous history that refined patron of the arts and enlightened lover of music and the drama the most noble the marquis of steyne <\s>
<s> his lordship's avocations as a statesman prevented him from attending the playhouse very often or coming very early <\s>
<s> but he occasionally appeared at the theatre in time for the ballet and was always received with the greatest respect by the manager from whom he sometimes condescended to receive a visit in his box <\s>

<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>
<s> one of the illustrious patrons of the museum theatre and occupant of the great proscenium box was a gentleman whose name has been mentioned in a previous history that refined patron of the arts and enlightened lover of music and the drama the most noble the marquis of steyne <\s>
<s> his lordship's avocations as a statesman prevented him from attending the playhouse very often or coming very early <\s>
<s> but he occasionally appeared at the theatre in time for the ballet and was always received with the greatest respect by the manager from whom he sometimes condescended to receive a visit in his box <\s>
<s> it communicated with the stage and when anything occurred there which particularly pleased him when a new face made its appearance among the coryphees or a fair dancer executed a pas with especial grace or agility mr <\s>

<s> he could not be seen by the audience for lord steyne sate modestly behind a curtain and looked only towards the stage but you could know he was in the house by the glances which all the corps de ballet and all the principal dancers cast towards his box <\s>

<s> bondi or sauterelle when they came down to earth curtsied and smiled especially to those hands before they walked up the stage again panting and happy <\s>

<s> it communicated with the stage and when anything occurred there which particularly pleased him when a new face made its appearance among the coryphees or a fair dancer executed a pas with especial grace or agility mr <\s>
<s> wagg or some other aide de camp of the noble marquis would be commissioned to go behind the scenes and express the great man's approbation or make the inquiries which were prompted by his lordship's curiosity or his interest in the dramatic art <\s>
<s> he could not be seen by the audience for lord steyne sate modestly behind a curtain and looked only towards the stage but you could know he was in the house by the glances which all the corps de ballet and all the principal dancers cast towards his box <\s>
<s> i have seen many scores of pairs of eyes as in the palm dance in the ballet of cook at otaheite where no less than a hundred and twenty lovely female savages in palm leaves and feather aprons were made to dance round floridor as captain cook ogling that box as they performed before it and have often wondered to remark the presence of mind of mademoiselle sauterelle or mademoiselle de bondi known as la petite caoutchoue who when actually up in the air quivering like so many shuttlecocks always kept their lovely eyes winking at that box in which the great steyne sate <\s>
<s> now and then you would hear a harsh voice from behind the curtain cry brava brava or a pair of white gloves wave from it and begin to applaud <\s>
<s> bondi or sauterelle when they came down to earth curtsied and smiled especially to those hands before they walked up the stage again panting and happy <\s>
<s> one night this great prince surrounded by a few choice friends was in his box at the museum and they were making such a noise and laughter that the pit was scandalised and many indignant voices were bawling out silence so loudly that wagg wondered the police did not interfere to take the rascals out <\s>

<s> it is a delicate matter entre nous une affaire de coeur <\s>

<s> there he sate then miserable and gazing at her <\s>
<s> and she took no more notice of him than he did of the rest of the house <\s>

<s> she spouted to admiration the few words of her part and looked it still better <\s>
<s> the eyes which had overthrown pen's soul rolled and gleamed as lustrous as ever but it was not to him that they were directed that night <\s>

<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>

<s> his lordship's avocations as a statesman prevented him from attending the playhouse very often or coming very early <\s>
<s> but he occasionally appeared at the theatre in time for the ballet and was always received with the greatest respect by the manager from whom he sometimes condescended to receive a visit in his box <\s>

<s> he was a tall and portly gentleman with a hooked nose and a profusion of curling brown hair and whiskers his coat was covered with the richest frogs braiding and velvet <\s>

<s> william minns without whom he never travelled <\s>

<s> dolphin's appearance in the remote little chatteris theatre may be accounted for in this manner <\s>
<s> in spite of all his exertions and the perpetual blazes of triumph coruscations of talent victories of good old english comedy which his play bills advertised his theatre which if you please and to injure no present susceptibilities and vested interests we shall call the museum theatre by no means prospered and the famous impresario found himself on the verge of ruin <\s>
<s> the great hubbard had acted legitimate drama for twenty nights and failed to remunerate anybody but himself the celebrated mr <\s>
<s> cawdor had come out in mr <\s>
<s> rawhead's tragedy and in their favourite round of pieces and had not attracted the public <\s>
<s> herr garbage's lions and tigers had drawn for a little time until one of the animals had bitten a piece out of the herr's shoulder when the lord chamberlain interfered and put a stop to this species of performance and the grand lyrical drama though brought out with unexampled splendour and success with monsieur poumons as first tenor and an enormous orchestra had almost crushed poor dolphin in its triumphant progress so that great as his genius and resources were they seemed to be at an end <\s>
<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>
<s> one of the illustrious patrons of the museum theatre and occupant of the great proscenium box was a gentleman whose name has been mentioned in a previous history that refined patron of the arts and enlightened lover of music and the drama the most noble the marquis of steyne <\s>
<s> his lordship's avocations as a statesman prevented him from attending the playhouse very often or coming very early <\s>
<s> but he occasionally appeared at the theatre in time for the ballet and was always received with the greatest respect by the manager from whom he sometimes condescended to receive a visit in his box <\s>
<s> it communicated with the stage and when anything occurred there which particularly pleased him when a new face made its appearance among the coryphees or a fair dancer executed a pas with especial grace or agility mr <\s>
<s> wagg or some other aide de camp of the noble marquis would be commissioned to go behind the scenes and express the great man's approbation or make the inquiries which were prompted by his lordship's curiosity or his interest in the dramatic art <\s>
<s> he could not be seen by the audience for lord steyne sate modestly behind a curtain and looked only towards the stage but you could know he was in the house by the glances which all the corps de ballet and all the principal dancers cast towards his box <\s>
<s> i have seen many scores of pairs of eyes as in the palm dance in the ballet of cook at otaheite where no less than a hundred and twenty lovely female savages in palm leaves and feather aprons were made to dance round floridor as captain cook ogling that box as they performed before it and have often wondered to remark the presence of mind of mademoiselle sauterelle or mademoiselle de bondi known as la petite caoutchoue who when actually up in the air quivering like so many shuttlecocks always kept their lovely eyes winking at that box in which the great steyne sate <\s>
<s> now and then you would hear a harsh voice from behind the curtain cry brava brava or a pair of white gloves wave from it and begin to applaud <\s>
<s> bondi or sauterelle when they came down to earth curtsied and smiled especially to those hands before they walked up the stage again panting and happy <\s>
<s> one night this great prince surrounded by a few choice friends was in his box at the museum and they were making such a noise and laughter that the pit was scandalised and many indignant voices were bawling out silence so loudly that wagg wondered the police did not interfere to take the rascals out <\s>
<s> wenham was amusing the party in the box with extracts from a private letter which he had received from major pendennis whose absence in the country at the full london season had been remarked and of course deplored by his friends <\s>

<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>
<s> one of the illustrious patrons of the museum theatre and occupant of the great proscenium box was a gentleman whose name has been mentioned in a previous history that refined patron of the arts and enlightened lover of music and the drama the most noble the marquis of steyne <\s>
<s> his lordship's avocations as a statesman prevented him from attending the playhouse very often or coming very early <\s>
<s> but he occasionally appeared at the theatre in time for the ballet and was always received with the greatest respect by the manager from whom he sometimes condescended to receive a visit in his box <\s>
<s> it communicated with the stage and when anything occurred there which particularly pleased him when a new face made its appearance among the coryphees or a fair dancer executed a pas with especial grace or agility mr <\s>

<s> he could not be seen by the audience for lord steyne sate modestly behind a curtain and looked only towards the stage but you could know he was in the house by the glances which all the corps de ballet and all the principal dancers cast towards his box <\s>
<s> i have seen many scores of pairs of eyes as in the palm dance in the ballet of cook at otaheite where no less than a hundred and twenty lovely female savages in palm leaves and feather aprons were made to dance round floridor as captain cook ogling that box as they performed before it and have often wondered to remark the presence of mind of mademoiselle sauterelle or mademoiselle de bondi known as la petite caoutchoue who when actually up in the air quivering like so many shuttlecocks always kept their lovely eyes winking at that box in which the great steyne sate <\s>
<s> now and then you would hear a harsh voice from behind the curtain cry brava brava or a pair of white gloves wave from it and begin to applaud <\s>
<s> bondi or sauterelle when they came down to earth curtsied and smiled especially to those hands before they walked up the stage again panting and happy <\s>
<s> one night this great prince surrounded by a few choice friends was in his box at the museum and they were making such a noise and laughter that the pit was scandalised and many indignant voices were bawling out silence so loudly that wagg wondered the police did not interfere to take the rascals out <\s>

<s> william minns without whom he never travelled <\s>

<s> dolphin's appearance in the remote little chatteris theatre may be accounted for in this manner <\s>
<s> in spite of all his exertions and the perpetual blazes of triumph coruscations of talent victories of good old english comedy which his play bills advertised his theatre which if you please and to injure no present susceptibilities and vested interests we shall call the museum theatre by no means prospered and the famous impresario found himself on the verge of ruin <\s>
<s> the great hubbard had acted legitimate drama for twenty nights and failed to remunerate anybody but himself the celebrated mr <\s>
<s> cawdor had come out in mr <\s>
<s> rawhead's tragedy and in their favourite round of pieces and had not attracted the public <\s>
<s> herr garbage's lions and tigers had drawn for a little time until one of the animals had bitten a piece out of the herr's shoulder when the lord chamberlain interfered and put a stop to this species of performance and the grand lyrical drama though brought out with unexampled splendour and success with monsieur poumons as first tenor and an enormous orchestra had almost crushed poor dolphin in its triumphant progress so that great as his genius and resources were they seemed to be at an end <\s>
<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>
<s> one of the illustrious patrons of the museum theatre and occupant of the great proscenium box was a gentleman whose name has been mentioned in a previous history that refined patron of the arts and enlightened lover of music and the drama the most noble the marquis of steyne <\s>
<s> his lordship's avocations as a statesman prevented him from attending the playhouse very often or coming very early <\s>
<s> but he occasionally appeared at the theatre in time for the ballet and was always received with the greatest respect by the manager from whom he sometimes condescended to receive a visit in his box <\s>
<s> it communicated with the stage and when anything occurred there which particularly pleased him when a new face made its appearance among the coryphees or a fair dancer executed a pas with especial grace or agility mr <\s>
<s> wagg or some other aide de camp of the noble marquis would be commissioned to go behind the scenes and express the great man's approbation or make the inquiries which were prompted by his lordship's curiosity or his interest in the dramatic art <\s>
<s> he could not be seen by the audience for lord steyne sate modestly behind a curtain and looked only towards the stage but you could know he was in the house by the glances which all the corps de ballet and all the principal dancers cast towards his box <\s>
<s> i have seen many scores of pairs of eyes as in the palm dance in the ballet of cook at otaheite where no less than a hundred and twenty lovely female savages in palm leaves and feather aprons were made to dance round floridor as captain cook ogling that box as they performed before it and have often wondered to remark the presence of mind of mademoiselle sauterelle or mademoiselle de bondi known as la petite caoutchoue who when actually up in the air quivering like so many shuttlecocks always kept their lovely eyes winking at that box in which the great steyne sate <\s>
<s> now and then you would hear a harsh voice from behind the curtain cry brava brava or a pair of white gloves wave from it and begin to applaud <\s>
<s> bondi or sauterelle when they came down to earth curtsied and smiled especially to those hands before they walked up the stage again panting and happy <\s>
<s> one night this great prince surrounded by a few choice friends was in his box at the museum and they were making such a noise and laughter that the pit was scandalised and many indignant voices were bawling out silence so loudly that wagg wondered the police did not interfere to take the rascals out <\s>
<s> wenham was amusing the party in the box with extracts from a private letter which he had received from major pendennis whose absence in the country at the full london season had been remarked and of course deplored by his friends <\s>

<s> dolphin's appearance in the remote little chatteris theatre may be accounted for in this manner <\s>
<s> in spite of all his exertions and the perpetual blazes of triumph coruscations of talent victories of good old english comedy which his play bills advertised his theatre which if you please and to injure no present susceptibilities and vested interests we shall call the museum theatre by no means prospered and the famous impresario found himself on the verge of ruin <\s>
<s> the great hubbard had acted legitimate drama for twenty nights and failed to remunerate anybody but himself the celebrated mr <\s>
<s> cawdor had come out in mr <\s>
<s> rawhead's tragedy and in their favourite round of pieces and had not attracted the public <\s>

<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>
<s> one of the illustrious patrons of the museum theatre and occupant of the great proscenium box was a gentleman whose name has been mentioned in a previous history that refined patron of the arts and enlightened lover of music and the drama the most noble the marquis of steyne <\s>
<s> his lordship's avocations as a statesman prevented him from attending the playhouse very often or coming very early <\s>
<s> but he occasionally appeared at the theatre in time for the ballet and was always received with the greatest respect by the manager from whom he sometimes condescended to receive a visit in his box <\s>
<s> it communicated with the stage and when anything occurred there which particularly pleased him when a new face made its appearance among the coryphees or a fair dancer executed a pas with especial grace or agility mr <\s>
<s> wagg or some other aide de camp of the noble marquis would be commissioned to go behind the scenes and express the great man's approbation or make the inquiries which were prompted by his lordship's curiosity or his interest in the dramatic art <\s>
<s> he could not be seen by the audience for lord steyne sate modestly behind a curtain and looked only towards the stage but you could know he was in the house by the glances which all the corps de ballet and all the principal dancers cast towards his box <\s>
<s> i have seen many scores of pairs of eyes as in the palm dance in the ballet of cook at otaheite where no less than a hundred and twenty lovely female savages in palm leaves and feather aprons were made to dance round floridor as captain cook ogling that box as they performed before it and have often wondered to remark the presence of mind of mademoiselle sauterelle or mademoiselle de bondi known as la petite caoutchoue who when actually up in the air quivering like so many shuttlecocks always kept their lovely eyes winking at that box in which the great steyne sate <\s>
<s> now and then you would hear a harsh voice from behind the curtain cry brava brava or a pair of white gloves wave from it and begin to applaud <\s>
<s> bondi or sauterelle when they came down to earth curtsied and smiled especially to those hands before they walked up the stage again panting and happy <\s>
<s> one night this great prince surrounded by a few choice friends was in his box at the museum and they were making such a noise and laughter that the pit was scandalised and many indignant voices were bawling out silence so loudly that wagg wondered the police did not interfere to take the rascals out <\s>
<s> wenham was amusing the party in the box with extracts from a private letter which he had received from major pendennis whose absence in the country at the full london season had been remarked and of course deplored by his friends <\s>

<s> it is a delicate matter entre nous une affaire de coeur <\s>
<s> there is a young friend of mine who is gone wild about a certain miss fotheringay an actress at the theatre here and i must own to you as handsome a woman and as it appears to me as good an actress as ever put on rouge <\s>

<s> he had under waistcoats many splendid rings jewelled pins and neck chains <\s>

<s> in spite of all his exertions and the perpetual blazes of triumph coruscations of talent victories of good old english comedy which his play bills advertised his theatre which if you please and to injure no present susceptibilities and vested interests we shall call the museum theatre by no means prospered and the famous impresario found himself on the verge of ruin <\s>

<s> he was dragging on his season wretchedly with half salaries small operas feeble old comedies and his ballet company and everybody was looking out for the day when he should appear in the gazette <\s>

<s> in spite of all his exertions and the perpetual blazes of triumph coruscations of talent victories of good old english comedy which his play bills advertised his theatre which if you please and to injure no present susceptibilities and vested interests we shall call the museum theatre by no means prospered and the famous impresario found himself on the verge of ruin <\s>
